LOS ANGELES, June 14.â 1n the j
cold, half-light of early morning

The husband, a vain, irresponsible,
character boy of twenty, crept
from the side of his sleeping girl wife
and took from 'a ante! a bottle of
chloroform. Behind him lay their baby,
less than a month old, asleep on a sofa
pillow Th, fumes of the drug applied ;
to the pale girl's nostrils deepened her

dental awakening. The murderer was
sure of his purpose and drew from be
hind him a shining revolver. The wife

husband. A shot was fired and the
young w.#man. her night garments

still smoking revolver to his body fired ;
a bullet through his abdomen. He fell j
to the floor, fatally wounded and was

pie that rushed to the scene.
Earl M. Hanehette, aged 20 years,

conceivable, even in a boy husband.
He railed weakly over the embarrass
ments that marital necessities placed
upon his ease-loving nature, and loafing
about their single room, quarreled pet
tishly with the girl he had sworn to |
honor and protect. He made no effort
to find employment, although he made ,
representations to the wife and to her j
nurse that he had secured, through an
: employment firm, a position as book
keeper in Santa Barbara. Hanehette
told them last night that he Intended to
leave for the coast town this morning.
The story of the prospective position
proves untrue. The wife was deeply

OPPOSITION
OF GERMANY
IS REPORTED
But American Delegates Yet
Hope to Maintain Their
Arbitration Plan.
Social Dispatch to The Call.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. WEL
LINGTON HOTEL, WASHINGTON,
June 14. â Notwithstanding the state-
ment to' the contrary made by Mr.
Holls, secretary of the American dele
gation .to The Hague conference, it is
I authoritatively stated the American
' delegation has reported determined op
j position by Germany to the adoption of
I an arbitration resolution by the confer-
ence.
Considerable regret is felt in official
circles at Germany's attitude, but the
authorities do not ccc what is to be
| gained by concealing the fact, as Mr.
Holls is evidently trying to do. It is
believed that what he meant to say Is
that Germany has not shown any op
position to arbitration in deliberations
jof the committee having arbitration In
charge.
So far ns this Government is con
cerned it has instructed Its delegates
to The Hague to press upon the confer
ence the acceptance of the American
arbitration proposition. The authorities
! have closely examined the several arbi
| tration plans advanced, and while nat-
Ural pride is taken in the American pro
posal it is asserted that in comparing
the merits of each that presented by
Enibassadur White and his associates,
as set forth in The Call .his morning,
is the best. The understanding at the
State Department is that the British
plan will be the basis of discussion
when the arbitration committee sub
mits a report to the. conference. The
American delegates will urge upon the
conference the adoption of the Ameri
can plan in its entirety, because it con
templates a. permanent arbitration tri
bunal, while leaving the powers inter
ested in any dispute the option nf pre
senting their controversy to it for ju
dicial settlement. The United States
would be willing to have changes made
in details connected with the appoint
ment of the tribunal, number of its
members, etc.. but the officials hope
that the general scheme will be
adopted.
THE HAGUE, June 14.â The refusal
"f Great Britain to accept the stipula
tions of the Brussels conference if they
are made definite will not prevent the
other powers from pledging themselves
to action in the matter. There is no
doubt that the Brussels conference sub
committee will reach a practical result,
but it is supposed that some of the
powers, while reserving the right tn ap
ply the new stipulations when neces
sary may not bind themselves forth
with.

INSECT INVADES
FIELDS OF GRAIN
Is Believed to Be the
Hessian Fly.
Pp<fia! Dispatch to ThÂ« Call.
SALINAS. June 14.â Ranchers in this.
section believe the much dreaded Hessian
fly has made Inroads into the wheat fields
of Monterey County. Duncan McKinnon
says he has discovered the pest, not only
on his own place but on adjoining farms.
Mr. McKinnon. who has hundreds of
acres of fine wheat and hariey. yesterday
brought to this city specimens of what
he believes to be larvae of the Hessian
fly. He finds the larvae at the joints of
wheat stalks. Th.- weakened stalks,
into by the insect, are broken by
the wind.
If it bo the genuine Hessian fly it is the j
first appearance of that pest in Califor- j
nia, so far as known. The description of I
the Hessian fly tits the specimens exhib- I
ited by Mr. McKinnon. They have be.'ii !
sent to an entomologist for examination. \
Whatever the insect Is, it is proving a i
plague to the wheat crops of this com- j
munity. J. Gardner examined the speci- !
mens tQ-day and asserts that they art:
the Hessian fly.
"1 am sure that the Hessian fly Is in :
my wheat," said Duncan McKinnon to
day. "I have 7"i acres in wheat, and I
find that every acre is affected My crop i
was tin.-. It would have made about fit
teen sacks to the acre, but now if I can j
get hay I will be satisfied. Up to three I
days ago I thought I would harvest a i
fine wheat crop. On- Tuesday I discbv- !
ered the insect pest, 1 had noticed for i
several days that my grain in spots was '
l ? lP â in)? ton rapidly. Going: into the!
field I found the grain in these particular
rspots had broken down. Naturally 1 ;
searched where the stalks had broken "and i
discovered the larvae. Since then I have â 
been searching diligently through all the ':
Other grain, but find that the pest has not ;
touched the barley, oats or other cereals. ;
When I had fluished with my crops I j
went over to adjoining ranches and dis- j
covered the pest there, too."
The female Hessian fly lays twenty or
thirty eggs in a crease of a leaf of the
growing wheat, and the larvae when j
hatched work their way between the leaf
and the stalk till they come to a joint,
a little below the surface, where they
suck the sap. They turn to pupae, en
closed in a covering; this is known as the I
â¢flaxseed" condition. The injury occa
sioned is not detected until the plant
grows higher. The usual result of the at
tack is that only shriveled heads of Â»rain
are formed.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JUNE 15, 1599.
AGREE ON TERMS
FOR THE MATCH
Jeffries and Sharkey
Eager to Meet.
INTERPOSING NO BARRIERS
THEIR MANAGERS TO CONFER
ON SATURDAY.
New Champion Determined to Give
All Ambitious Aspirants a
Chance to Win the
Honors.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK. June 14.â Everything now
points to a match between James Jeffrie's
and Thomas Bharkey for' the. heavy
weight championship of- the world. 'Wil
liam .A. Brady, Jeffries' manager, and
Thomas O'Rourke,' the adviser of the
sailor pugilist, will have a meeting on
Saturday, and. there is little doubt that
the managers will come to an understand
ing with regard to a match.
Some of the conditions made by Jef
fries in his original challenge to Sharkey
and to which O'Rourke objected on behalf
of the sailor will likely be modified.
Brady said to-day Jeffries would- waive
the side bet of $10,000, owing to the fact
that a Bide wager was considered illegal.
O'Rourke. at first objected to George Slier
of Chicago as referee, but he has decided
to waive his objections to Siler and will
accept the Chicago sporting writer as the
referee of the contest.
Brady intimated that theatrical ar
rangements would be a secondary con
sideration with the new champion. The
big fellow is anxious to fight all comers,
and they will be given consideration in
due time.
Sharkey will be given the first chance,
because Jeffries feels none too kindly dis
posed toward the sailor on account of the
latter's utterances in regard to their fight
In San Francisco. â¢â¢>
Still another aspirant to heavy-weight
honors is shaking his money at Jeffries.
He is Mike Morrissey. a Tipperary giant,
who Is soon to meet Peter Maher at the
I.enox Athletic Club. Morrissey's man
ager "P. J. Dwyer, has deposited $2riflo with
George Considine to bind a match with
JeJtrlts. Should the Irishman dispose of
Maher in good shape he might.be able to
force a meeting between the champion
and himself, but if he falls an easy prey
to Maher's science, as the general public
expects, he will receive scant notice from
Jeffries.

Men Needed in the Fields.
WOODLAND, June 14.â The scarcity of
man who will work in the. harvest field
has become a serious problem for the
farmers There have been many inquiries
to-day but there are but few idle men in
the county. There will be much difficul
ty in getting reliable help to harvest the
crops Where heretofore a great many
idle men found their way from San Fran
cisco and other big cities into the country
about harvest time, the farmers this year
have been compelled to rely upon labor
ers who make their homes in the country
the year around.
Employment for Whites.
WOODLAND, June 14.â The rumor that
the fruit crop of the Tolo orchard has
been sold on the trees is not true. Some
canning stock and also some fruit for
Eastern shipment have already been con
tracted for, but the company will handle
the crop and prepare it for shipment.
The report that but little white help
would be needed In the orchard this sea
son seems to be equally unfounded. More
help probably will be needed than ever
before, and where It Is possible to obtain
reliable help It will be given the prefer-
Will Run No Excursions.
WOODLAND, June 14.â The proposed
excursion between Marysville and Wood
land on the Fourth of July has been
abandoned. On account of the great de
mand for cars the railroad company has
decided to run no excursion trains on that
day.
H. O. HAVEMEYER
EXPLAINS CAUSE
OF THE TRUSTS
Says It Is the Government,
Through Tariff Laws, That
Plunders the People.
WASHINGTON, 'June 14.â Henry 0. ,
Havemeyer,' president of the American;
Sugar Kenning Company, was; examined;
by the- Industrial Commission". to-day in
connection with its investigation of trusts.
His .testimony related" almost wholly to:
the sugar industry, and he opened with a
vigorous attack upon the customs tariff,
Which he declared was the "mother of all
trusts," becoming such' by providing '."in
ordinate protection to manufactured arti
cles." . . :
The full text of the statement read by
Mr. tiavemeyer is as follows:
' "The. mother of all trusts is the â  customs
tariff bill. The existing bill and the preced
ing ones have been the occasion tor the forma
tion of -ill the large trusts, with very few ex
ceptions, inasmuch as they provide for an in
ordinate protection to all' the Interests of the
country â sugar refining excepted. Economic ad
vantages incident to the consolidation of large
Interests In the same line of business are a
great Incentive to their formation, but these ,
bear a very Insignificant proportion to the ad
vantages granted In the way of protection un
der the customs tariff.
"There probably la not an industry that re
quires a protection of more than 10 per cent
ad valorem, and it Is to obtain what is provided
over such' percentage In the tariff that leads
to the formation of what are commonly spoken
of as 'trusts. ' , â¢ . â¢ â¢ â 
"With a protection to an industry not ex- ,
oeedlng 10 per cent, all menace to the com
munity of trusts would cease. This 10 per cent
would represent the difference- in cost of pro
duct lore and likewise act as a protection against
surplus products of foreign countries being
dumped in bur local markets, thereby interfering
with the regular and economic working of our
industries. Any advantage that might then
accrue to such combinations they would be
fully' entitled to and the public would not be
damaged thereby, as any expansion of price
would.be met by foreign competition and re
â¢ i have said that sugar was an exception.
The rat.- of protection on sugar is an eighth
of a cent per pound, which is about 3 1 ,s per
cent ad valorem, and is not the difference in
the cost of refining between this and foreign
countries. The least It should have is 8 per
cent, or in specific figures 1.4 cents per
pound. The sugar refining industry of this
country no matter what form its organisation
is entitled to adequate protection it any in
dustry is." There are at least 100,000 l Pie de
pendent upon it. What It pays or has paid to
Its stockholders in the past represents nothing
more than a fair return to the capital in
vested considering the extent of the business.
â¢â¢The United States tariff bill, in assessing
about $40 per ton duty on imported sugar,
pays into the pockets of a few* Louis laiians
on their annual crop of 250.000 tons. $.iO,0(K).OuO
to the Hawaiian Islanders, probably represented
hv 150 foreigners, on their annual crop of _-â >â¢â 
000 tons. Sio.fiOO.OOii. say 100.000 tons produced
elsewhere In the United States, $4,000,000. Here
you have 84.000 extracted from the people
of the United States for the sake of getting
the revenue which HO pel ton on foreign sugar
provides. This Is merely illustrative of the
whole tariff every line of it -and its effect upon
the people. In fact the tariff bill clutches the
people by the throat and then Governors and
the Attorney Generals of the Southern Mates
take action, not against the cause, but against
the machinery which the people employ to rifle
1 '"There appears to be In the public mind a
distinction between robbery by an individual
and thai by a corporation. What is com
mendable in an Individual appears to be dis
honest In a corporation. 1 maintain that it is
Immaterial to the public In what form business
is on Â« whether by an individual, a firm, a
corporation or even the trust. These are mere
ly forms of conducting business, or In other
words, â  machinery tor the operation Of busi
ness It is the duty of the Government to
see that under the tariff laws they enact this
machinery cannot, In its action, result to the
detriment or impoverishment of the public.
"It would have been very easy and proper
for the Government to have put a correspond
ing internal revenue tax on sugar simul
taneously with the customs duty, and have
these 524000 000 which now go Into the pockets
of a few" individuals, go Into the trwinury for
the benefit of the whole people. If the **-
pend of the Government needed to be provided
for an-1 protection to American Industries was
desired the imposition of 10 per cent ad valor
em or Tall manufactured products would have
settled the matter There is very reason
If revenue is desired, that with t a duty on
raw materials of foreign product, a corre
sponding internal revenue should be Â» evl *<LÂ°n
such products and the protection of manu
facturers thereof limited to an additional 10
'^YVerefit that all this agitation against
trusts against merely the business <%****%*
employed to take from the public *)Â» at "Â£
Government In its tariff laws says It Is Proper
and suitable they should have. It is the Gov
ernment, through He tariff laws Menplun
ders the people, and the trusts, etc.. are merelj
the machinery for doing it.
"The statements made before the committee
in Congress and the speeches against the
sugar refining- company are absolutely devoid
of truth-utterly unworthy of credit. The In
tention of Congress, however, to enact some
n?ng"nto law contrary to what the sugar re
fining industry property required resulted, ir
respective of the great injury and injustice
done in the passage of the existing sugar
schedule. Whether the sugar refining company
haÂ« been a benefit or Injury to the community
can be best expressed in a table showing the
prices for a considerable period prior to the
formation of the trust and lor a correspond
lnÂ«r d. d ffe r U enc^ U sfnce its formation shows an
advantage to the consumerâ note that, con
sumer' - of over one-eighth of a cent per pound
What the sugar company has disbursed in divi
i dends has been not because of any extreme
protection under the tariff, but out of condi
tions perfectly legitimate and commendable.
The record Shows conclusively that no advan
tage has ever been taken by the. company of
any protection in excess of the 8 per cent
Claimed, that is, 1.4 cents per pound, under the
- "Tariff laws should not afford protection ex
ceeding 10 per cent to any industry, so that,
Irrespective of the form of doing business, In
dividual or corporation, the people, that is,
the consumer would be protected. As the tariff
laws are and have been it Is the consumer,
that is the great mass of the people, who has
terribly suffered to the great advantage of the
few of the business community.
"The sugar company undoubtedly tries Its
utmost to enlarge Its business, but does it in
a. way which it considers the only proper one,
that is. by making the price so low as to defy
competition.
"It is my opinion that corporations are under
no obligation whatever to any of the States for
their existence. Quite the reverse; the States
are under obligations to them. If the plants
of the industries consolidated were capable of
being put on wheels and moved from State to
State, you would find very active bidding for
them. It is not an unusual thing for certain*
localities to guarantee free taxation for twenty
years; free water and in some instances, give
the land to corporations to have them organize
under their statutes and locate In their States.
"Hostility to capital meets with its own con
demnation. That is illustrated by the situa
tion in New York. where a discrimination
against capital has prevented its employment
and driven it elsewhere, resulting in a greatly
Increased tax rate and a tendency to Increase
the number of unemployed. â¢
"All I have to say about trade organizations
and strikes is that, without violence, they are
natural. They have one objection, however,
their tendency to reduce all labor to a low level.
Business is not philanthropy. Capital and labor
will adjust their own relations If they are let
alone. Interference always operates against
one or the other. That means to the disad
vantage of both. There Is no such thing as
monopoly In these days except that which re
sults from patents and copyrights.
"The true â communism -of pelf is the cus
toms tariff bill. It says to the people: 'Here
is the law we have enacted for your robbery.
T>o not complain of It, but do your utmost to
attack and Injure the machinery engaged In
extracting from you what we legislate shall
be taken from you. Keep up the clatter while
the voters on the tariff bill take advantage of
the noise and enact laws that cause your Im
poverishment and thus contribute to the greed
and avarice of the few.'
"There Is. a prevailing hostility to wealth.
This is perfectly Illogical. Every one wants
money. It is the abuse of money, not Its pos
session, which is opposed to public . Interest.
This hostility ' finds its outlet in hostile legis
lation, "In "unequal and unjust : taxation. All
this is probably unconstitutional. It Is di
rectly against the Interest of the very classâ
I mean the poorer classâ whom it is supposed
to benefit. " â¢
"Corporations, whether directly such or In
the form of trusts, are an expedient for unit-
Ing the interest of a large number of per-"
sons of smaller â¢ means. Into a large aggrega
tion of capital. Attack upon them Is. there
fore, an attack upon â  their stockholders. In
the care of many well-conducted corporations
the stockholders- are very numerous and are
often persons of moderate â¢ means, dependent
upon their Income for their : support. - - .:
"In the absence of all disturbing causes the
direct tendency of a combination of capital is
to promote economy, reduce expenses and â  di
minish price. This does not mean that a per
son having- anything to pell will not get for
It the largest price that he can. It. means
that with the abundance of capital ready for
investment, which Is always found every
where, the only way to prevent- competition
Is to keep prices below, the competitive â¢ point.
"Great â â  public Improvements. - ; factories . and
other enterprises requiring large capital either
are Impossible unless through the instrumen
tality -of corporations or " are â¢; possible only
'â¢â  AUSTIN,- Tex., June-14.â Attorney
General Smith to-day issued in
structions to Secretary of State
I lardy " that' hereafter no " corpora
tion chartered' under the laws of
New Jersey for the. purpose of do
ing-, business.- in Texas shall be
granted a permit to do business in
this State. Attorney General
Smith bases his action on the
ground that the granting of such
permit is against public policy,
and that it is. for the public good
that such corporations be excluded
from the State.
through the action of Individuals, themselves
possessed of unlimited capital. U Is- easy to
siv what, In the latter case, would happen to
the community.
"One form in which hostile action manifests
Itself is by legislation which is intended to
keep corporations and their products out of
particular States. This legislation in some
States takes the form of bills which permit
citizens to buy goods and with impunity to
refuse to pay for them. This is a premium on
dishonesty. In other States the attempt is to
exclude articles which are of. prime necessity.
If the producer? of such articles were to follow
the lead and to combine to keep their com
modities out of the State it is easy to see
what would result, i. c., either the people
would be compelled to go without or they
Would be forced to pay an inordinate price.
"Trade will always take care of itself If it
is left to pursue ordinary channels. A w^ll
see to it that B does not have any extraordi
nary advantages. It Is only when the State
Interferes a situation is created of which an
advantage can be taken against the interest of
the community.
"In these days there are two forms and only
two forms of monopoly. One, that which re
sults from a patent and copyrights. It if* unl
vi-rsnlly recognized that this la In the Interest,
not against the Interest of the public. The
other, that which comes from . unfair tariff
discrimination.
"Tariff for revenue need not be considered.
The expenses of the Government must of course
be provided for. Tariff for the purpose of
equalizing against foreign bounties or foreign
discrimination does not need to be Justified.
Beyond that there is no excuse for giving to
one industry a protection of ]00 per cent, as
against 4 per cent for another, or any more
than 10 per cent. The result is that the Gov
ernment fleeces the community at large in the
Interest of some favored Industry.
"It must be kept in mind that this is a rich
country, not a poor country. It must be kept
In mind that the object of every one is to make

FRESNO GROWERS
SWING INTO LINE
Pledge Aid to the State
Organization.
SUBSCRIPTIONS FOR STOCK
SUPPORT THE REFRIGERATOR
LINE SCHEME.
Mass Meeting of Orchardists Aroused
to Enthusiasm by the Words of
Executive Committee
Members.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
FRESNO, June 14.â mass meeting of
deciduous fruit growers was held here to
day to arouse enthusiasm in support of
the association recently organized at Sftc
ramento. R. I). Stephens, president of the
executive committee, was present and
with him were the other members of the
committee. Alexander Gordon of this city
was elected chairman after me meeting
had been called to order. Mr. Gordon
called upon R. D. Stephens, who ex
plained the proposed plan of escape from
the clutches of the refrigerator car com-, /
parties. X W
W. E. Levdall of Sacramento read the
proposed articles of incorporation, which
were to the effect that the corporation
was to be for a term of tiff, ears; the
capital stock to be $600,000, and the direc
tors to be nine- in number. The principal
place of business will be Sacramento The,
capital stock being limited, subscribers
will be liable for ojiiy the amount of stock
for which they subscribe.
Edward I. Galvin, treasurer of the as
sociation, cave an interesting talk on th^
necessity of organization. He thought th i
attempt of the organization was the wis
est plan that had ever been placed before
the fruit growers. The situation was crit
ical. Many who would like to join with
other growers and form this organization
could not do so because they were crip
pled â their crops being mortgaged to
f*iit-shipping concerns. .The fruit grow
ers themselves were the capitalists, and
not the shipping concerns nor the Eastern
capitalists. All that was needed was or
ganization. .
The horticultural business of the State
was greater, he said, than that of any
other State, and counted far into the mil
lions and was steadily growing. Consid
ering this, it was foolish to think the
fruit growers could not be independent if
they wished. Mr. Galvin's was a forcible
argument and made a deep impression. .
- Mr. Stephens followed Mr. Galvin. He '
spoke in favor of organization, declaring
that if the association were successful the
rates on green fruit shipments to the
East would be reduced from 50 to 70 per
cent, and that this amount would go di
rectly into the hands of the growers.
After the meeting an opportunity was
given the growers to sign contracts ami
many of them did so. The actual signers
numbered over a dozen and assurances . .
were received from many more. A com- \ '
mittee consisting of F. R. Lindsay. Johm
S. Dore, Frank Rowell, R. R. Madsen
and L. H. Twede was appointed to ar
range for another mass meeting a week
from next Saturday, to work up more
enthusiasm on the subject of organiza
tion. The growers here are heartily in
favor of the plan adopted at Sacramento
and may be depended upon to support the
association. ~r'T ."

UPPER YUKON RIVER
OPEN FOR NAVIGATION
PORT TOWNSEND, June 14.â The Tp
per Yukon River is now open for naviga
tion. The steamer Flora, whirh left Daw
son on May 25, arrived at White Horse !
on June 3. One of her passengers. Wll-
Ham P. Allen of Seattle, who arrived here
to-night on the steamer Al-Ki, reports
that the Yukon River was open at Daw
son on May 17â nine days later than last '
year. The trip was made without diffi
culty until Lake Marsh was reached.
There the ice had to be cut in order to
get through. He reports forty-two loaded
BCOWS ai Tagish. lietween Tagish and
Bennett fifty more scows were passed, all
laden and bound for Dawson.
Since the big (ire Dawson has been the
scene of business activity. Sawmills have
been running day sind n'ght, but have
been unable to supply the demand fur
lumber. Ev^ry man who can drive a nail
has secured employment.
The season was somewhat backward
this year, and as a result thf> clean-up
did not commence until a short time be-'
fnre Allen started out. He says what
little was known of the results of thr>
clean-up was most satisfactory and claim
owners were jubilant. Many predict that
the output of gold this year will far ex
ceed the expectations of the most san
guine.
Allen says the reported exodus from
Dawson for Cape Nome is mythical, an
news of the strike at that place had not
reached Dawson when he left.

OUTLAW MOORE
SLEEPS IN COURT
Utterly Indifferent as to
His Fate.
Special I>iÂ«;iatph to Thp <"all.
NEVADA CITY, June 14.â Edward
Moore went sound asleep this morning
during the progress of his trial for the
murder of Officer Kilroy. His face was
as placid as that of an infant. He slept
about three minutes, then awoke with
such a start that he almost tumbled off
his chair. If he has realization of the
seriousness of his position he does not
manifest it. He Is either inhumanly In
different to his fate or is a consummate
actor.
The impaneling of a jury was not rom
pleted till late t His afternoon. A Bcore of
freeholders who were examined as to their
qualifications swore that they were sn
firm in their conviction as to Moore's
guilt, because of what they had read of
his crime In the papers, thai not enough
testimony on the lines of insanity fir self
defense could be piled up by his lawyer to
cause them to ohange their minds. One
of these when asked if he could not give
Moore a fair trial, said nothing could be
more to his taste than to sit onÂ«the case In
Judge Lynch's court. Others declared that
if they were ki pt nil ihe jury and the plea
<>f insanity were interposed they would
treat it as a sham and a subterfuge.
In his opening statement the District
Attorney said he would prove that Moore
had on various occasions warned Kilroy
not to attempt his arrest if he did not
want to die. and that he had told others
he would kill the policeman the first time
he caught him alone.
Dr. A. 11. Tickeil described the three
powder-burned wounds in the dead man,
any one of which would have been fatal.
County Surveyor Waggoner showed a dia
gram of the scene of the killing, and B.
A. Moore, a photogrpher, identified the
pictures he had taken of the wounds the
morning they were inflicted.
While the wounds were being described
to the jury the dead man's brother, Jo
seph, a big stalwart miner, bowed his
head and wept.

SUNSET COMPANY
SUED BY D. O. MILLS
Damages Demanded for the Buining
of Trees by Telephone
Linemen.
REDWOOD (ITY. June 14.â D. O. Mills
| commenced an action in the Superior
Court yesterday against the Sunset Tele
phone am! Telegraph Company. The suit
will be a notable one. because it will de
termine the rights of the people against
a corporation.
Mr. Mills is the owner of n large tract
of land in the first township of this coun
ty, fronting on the county road, along
Which he lias planted numerous shade
and ornamentkj trees. The employes of
the Telephone and Telegraph Company.
in harfging wires, have cut and marred
the trees to such an extent as to almost
cause their ruin. Mr. Mills lias asked
the court to restrain the company from
further destroying his property, and sues
for Slihhi damages already done. Judge
Buck issued a restraining order yester
day. The trial is looked forward to with
great interest, especially by property
owners who have devoted much time anil
expense in beautifying the public high
ways in the improvement of their prop
erty.
To Discuss Irrigation.
MARYSVTLLE, June 14.â One week
from to-day a public meeting will be hold
in Browns Valley to consider matters in
connection with the irrigation district.
Water arriving in the ditch must be
cared for and distributed. To do this
money must lie raised, either by taxation
or by" the sale of the water. Recent court !
decisions have left the organization under |
a cloud. The policy advocated by the
National Irrigation Congress of Federal
storage reservoirs and leasing public
grazing lands to settlers on adjacent
lands will be discussed. The meeting will
be addressed by George H. Maxwell,
chairman of the executive committee of
the National Irrigation Association.
Every one interested in the prospertiy of
those" engaged in the, stockrasing industry
is urged to attend.

teen minutes of the time she struck all

ENCYCLICAL LETTER
ARRIVES TOO LATE
NEW YORK. June 14.â The encyclical
letter of Pope Leo XIII dedicating the en- !
tire world to the sacred heart of Jesus |
and designating June 9. 10 and 11 to be |
set apart throughout the Christian world ;
for special devotion, has just reached this |
country.
Owing to some unaccountable delay on j
the part ot the authorities at Rome it j
failed to reached Monfeignor Martinelli in
time for the Archbishops and Bishops of j
the United States to act in accordance j
with its stipulations. It was designed to |
be read in parishes throughout the cnun- |
Try and promulgated in sufficient time to;
make the consecration of widespread in- i
tereot and a public demonstration not un- |
like that at Vienna last Sunday, when the
Emperor solemnly dedicated himself,
family and court to the sacred heart.
The encyclical appeared first in the
Observatore Romano, the official organ
of the Vatican. May 26. and made Its way
t<i P;iris. Cologne, Vienna and London in |
time for due observance. Archbishop Cor- I
rigan has been waiting its receipt, and
upon its non-arrival he Issued a pastoral
letter to the clergy of this diocese, and on !
the feast of the sacred heart. June 9, in
lieu of the act of consecration specially
recommended by the Pope in his encycli
cal the Archbishop recited the act that
has been in use the last quarter of "a
century.
The encyclical is vet news to a large
part of the Bishops of the United Stales.
When His Holiness learns of the delay he
will in all probability give an extension of
time for cansecration. Since the feast of
the blessed Margaret Mary, with whom
the devotion originated, falls on October
17, it is posible the Holy Father will set
that day paart for the dedication of this
country.
DISPLACEMENT OF THE
HEART CAUSES DEATH
SALINAS, June 14,â Dr. G. TV. Caldwell.
a dentist and large property owner of
this valley, died yesterday. Dr. Caldwell's
Illness was a long one and most peculiar,
his case puzzling the medical fraternity
of the State. Originally he had a pul
monary complaint, but it seemed to de
velop Into lympanona, a virtual displace
ment of the heart from its proper pasl
'â¢â  tion to the right side of the body. No cure
â  was found, and several times the patient
'â  remained in a comatose condition for a
long time. Dr. Caldwell was a prominent
Native Son, unmarried and quite wealthy.
RATTLESNAKE'S BITE
KILLS NOTED VAQUERO
UKIAH, June 14.â Juan Sppulvedn. a
noted vaquero of Round Valley, was bit
ten by a rattlesnake on Monday, and.
after suffering intensely for four hours,
died in great agony. Sepulveda had been
in the habit nf catching- rattlers by the
nccU and clinking them to death. He ne
cured an enormous one on Monday after
noon, but his grip was too far back. The
snake turned its head and sunk Its fangs
into the vaquero's index finger. Sepul
veda was a pioneer of Northern Mendo
cino. having immigrated hither before
Mendoiino County was formed.

Fell Thirty Feet.
Michael l,avin, a hod-carrier, GS years
Of age. fell from the roof of a building at
Beale and Harrison streets a few minutes
before 8 o'clock last 'light and was picked
up unconscious. He was taken to the
Harbor Receiving Hospital, where it was
found that he had sustained a fracture of
the skull and possible Internal injuries.
His recovery is doubtful. Nobody seems
to know just how the accident occurred.
Lavtn went to work on the building at
about 2 o'clock yesterday afternoon and
workc-d steadily ap to the time the acci
dent occurred. He was not seen to fall,
but it Is supposed that he missed his
footing while stepping from the topmost
rung of the ladder to the roof. His place
of residence is 37 1 * Madison avenue, and
he bus a wife and married daughter.

Santa Cruz will bump up against the
Senators at Sacramento and is liable to
have a distressing time of it now that
the tailenders have got to moving. San
Jose will go to "Watsonville, and that se
ries will be for blood from the sound-off.
Manager Harris frankly admits that he
would rather go against any team in the
league than the Senators. The way
those tailenders put it on Hank's men
in all sorts of extra-inning skyrocket
games makes the local magnate exceed
ingly tired. The other teams seem to
have no great difficulty in vanquishing
the Statesmen, but with Harris it is dif
ferent. Hank says that if he loses the
championship it will be due to Sacra
mento.
The sirocco that swept over Recreation \
Park during last Sunday's game, strang- i
ling and blinding the spectators with
thick clouds of sand, has stirred the man- i
agement to take steps to alleviate the dts- !
comforts of its patrons. The diamond has !
been harrowed and scraped off and 100 !
loads of rich loam Is being spread, which I
will pack down smooth and solid, so that I
there will he no further annoyance from i
dust and dirt. Incidentally it will improve I
the work of the inflelders, as they can I
handle the ball much better on a loam !
diamond than on the sand. This Improve- i
ment will be completed in ample time fur â 
Saturday's game. In case the manage- I
ment docs not secure more corrrmodiLns
grounds for next season the entire Held ,
at Recreation Park will be covered with
loam in the fall and the outfield up to the j
I

Since Dr. McGraw took hold of the ban
Jose team that town has become one or
the best ball towns in the State in point
of attendance and Interest In the .game.
The team is steadily improving in na
work, the members are all popular wit n
the public, and the Garden Citys bid lair
to show a handsome balance on the
credit side of the ledger at the close or
the season. Last Sunday the team put up
a wonderfully fast game against the Lob
sters, the score standing 1 to 1 for nine
Innings. In the tenth, however, the lead
ers won out with another tally.
Sullivan will be back in the game this
week. His ankle, which he sprained last
Sunday morning, is greatly Unproved.
The locals will need his batting in the
Oakland series.
By mutual consent Sacrampnto and
Santa Cruz have exchanged dates. The
Senators were scheduled to play at the
sea beach this week and (he Lobsters
were due in Sacramento on July 22 and '.',.
Now, however, Santa Cruz will go to Sac
ramento this week and the Senators will
make their trip in July.
Although rumors still float up from
Wataonvllle of a change in the manage
ment, Mr. Struve is said to have recon
sidered his determination to sell out and
will remain in charge of the team.

and Secretary Bonnet, who constitute
the arbitration board of the California
league, will meet in a few days and pass
upon all the protests now pending.

MILITARY RULE IN
IDAHO IS DENOUNCED
BIG MASS-MEETING AT METRO-
POLITAN HALL.
Labor and Building Trades Councils
Adopt Strong Resolutions â Prom
inent Speakers Voice Their
Sentiments.
Metropolitan Temple was crowded last
night in response to a rail fnr a mass
mer-ting held under the auspices of the
Labor Council, Building Tr.i.icp Council
and Affiliated Unions. The object of thn
meeting wns to voice a strong protest
against the establishment of military rule
;it Wardner, [daho, the scene of the re
cent miners' troubles.
Chairman P. U. McCarthy stated that
it was not a partisan or political protest
nut one in the name of humanity against
the methods employed by the Idaho au
thorities and General Mefriam.
Judge Highton, James Q. Maguire and
A. R. Andre addressed the audience in a
similar vein, and the following resolu
tions were adopted by unanimous vote:
Whereas. The horrors of the Ward
ner "bull pen" and the unconstitu
tional arts of Governor Steunenberg
and Brigadier General Merriam in
their treatment of the parties sus
pected of complicity in the destruction
of the Bunker Hill and Sullivan Hill
mines at Wardner, Idaho, continue in
spitp of protests from fill parts of the
country; and
Whereas. It is evident from the most
reliable sources thnt Governor Steun
enberg is a pliant tool of the mine
owners, combined to establish indus
trial feudalism in Idaho; therefore
be it
Resolved. By the citizens' mass- I
meeting called by the Labor Council
and Building Trades Council of San
Francisco that we protest against the
suspension of the writ of habeas cor
pus in time of peace; that we denounce
the suppression of free speech and
free press and the denial of "the right
of combination"; that we inndemn the
wanton brutalities of the "bull pen"
and that we call upon all trades
unionists and citizens in general to
make similar protest; and further
Resolved That we call upon Presi
dent McKinley. as commander-in-chief
of the army, to order the cessation of
the usurpation of power by the mili
tary; and further
Resolved, That we request the Cali
fornia Representatives to ;isk. on the
assembling of Congress, for a Congres
sional Investigation of the Wardner
brutalities; and further
Resolved. That the secretary of this
meeting wire these resolutions to
President McKinley.
Shu Francisco, CaL, June 14, 1899.
A telegram from Patrick Reddy, who is |
THE SAS FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JUNE 15, 1899.
now in Idaho, was read, in which he re
iterated the statements which were re
cently published in The Call regarding the
situation at Wardner.
"The lives and liberties of the miners
and laborers," he concludes, "are now in
the hands of one manâ the executive of
Idaho, who has allied himself with their
adversaries and who has called in United
States troops to carry out the schemes
of the mine owners to increase their own
enormous profits by pauperizing, oppress
ing and starving the miners into abject
and absolute submission."
Mayor Phelan and Charles 1,. Fatten
sent letters of regret at being unable to
be present, while sympathizing with the
purpose for which the mass-meeting was
called.

C. J. TRUMAN TESTIFIES.
Said Captain Hall Recognized Rulof
son as His Son.
T. J. Truman was called as a witness
yesterday in the action instituted by A.
I . Rulofpon, in which he seeks to re
cover the entire estate of the late Cap-
tain Wlnslow O. Hall, whom the plaintiff
claims was his adopted fater. Mr. Tru
man said that on many occasions when
he met Captain Hall he would speak to
him about Rulofson, when the witness
said Captain Hall always referred to as
his adopted son. Captain Hall said that
he had legally adopted Rulofson. Mr. Tru
man said, and also said that he always
intended to care for him and treat him
as he would his own son. Mr. Truman
was positive in his statements and was
an important witness for the plaintiff.

DEMOCRATS SHOW NO REGARD FOR
REPRESENTATION OF MINORITY.
Delegates to the Convention to
Be Selected by Districts.
THE disgruntled "uitlandors" of the I
local Democracy marie their con
templated raid last night into the i
territory of the Committee of One
Hundred. They met their Kruegprs
dorp and were routed by the Boers under
the leadership of "Onm" Jasper McDon
ald. Major Barna McKinnie led the
charge, encouraging Ills followers by wav
ing a resolution calling for the enlarge
mi'iit of the Democratic Volksraad that
the privilege of a vote therein should lie
extended to at least fifty of the faithful
now in the hinterlands.
The major was ably supported by Ben
Wright and one or two others, but ijis
forces were impaled on chevaux do frise,
planted in the name of points of order by
"Oom" Loo Kaufman, and wqre allowed
to wriggle there, while "Oom" Jasper held
the disturbing resolution up in the air.
This action settles once for all, no mat
ter how much the "uitlanders" hope
otherwise, the Question of the enlarge
ment of the committee. The members do
not wish to confer any of their powers on
outsiders, and their vole last night shows
that they are sufficiently in the majority
to shut off any attempt to Increase their j
number.
The committee also decided on district,
as against precinct, election of delegates
to the coming convention. This was not
done, however, without a vigorous pro- |
tesj from several members, who claimed '
that the minority had sonif rights. The
committee also adopted the report of the;
committee of seven on apportionment.
This was not done without considerable
difference of opinion and discussion,
some acrimony and many appeals to the
fairness of the committee.
Probably the most important business
of the meeting was transacted just before
adjournment, when a resolution was in
troduced that the chairman of the com
mittee be empowered to appoint from its
membership an executive committee of
eighteen, one from each Assembly Dis
trict, with the chairman and secretary
added. to engage headquarters and use
all means necessary to enlist Democratic
voters in the campaign. Although the evi
dent purpose of the resolution was to put
the power of the entire Committee of
One Hundred in the hands of the twenty,
it was adopted with a rush. The chair
was given until the next meeting to make
up his mind.
It was 8:50 o'clock when Chairman Jas
per McDonald ascended the platform and
rapped the. committee to order. After
rollcall and the approval of the minutes
of the last meeting Secretary Walsh read
a communication from the Democratic
State Central Committee, heretofore pub
lished, urging the Committee of One
Hundred to so act in the apportionment
of delegates to the county convention as
to promote harmony in the party in this
county.
Pat Boland declared that the communi
cation was an unwarranted interference
with the affairs of the local committee,
and moved that the communication be re
turned to the State Central Committee.
\V J. McGee offered as an amendment
to the motion that the communication be
received and placed on rile. The amend
ment carried.

mittee appointed to learn whether any
Democratic office-holders were aiding or
abetting the movement to test the validity
of the charter, reported that assurances
had been received from all Democrats
elected atÂ»the last election declaring that
they were not parties to any such suit,
and that they believed in sustaining the

lan Supervisors Byington. Deasy and
Lackmann, School Director Eells and
Brandensteln, Judges Conlan and Slogan,
Coroner Hill. Assessor Dodge and City
and County Attorney Lane to this effect.

lutions were embodied, was then adopted:

The League Begins Active Steps for
Its Erection.
The. general committee of the .Lincoln
Monument League met last night in the
rooms of the Union League Club, Palace
Hotel, with President W. W. Stone in
the chair and Frank Elliott Myers secre
tary.
During the past year the Span
ish war has absorbed every interest and
comparatively little progress ; has been
made, but now the league will make re
newed efforts to accomplish the erection
of a monument to the memory of Lincoln
in Golden Gate Park.
The committee has had assurances from
wealthy citizens that just as soon as. suf
ficient progress had been made toward
the accumulation of a generous . fund
they would come forward with liberal
contributions.

ADVERTISEMENTS.
When we read of an elephant fiunter who
has been trampled to death in the wilds of
India, we wonder at the foolhardiness of a
man who will travel round the world and
endure all manner of hardships, in order to
court death in a far away jungle. A man
does not have to make a journey to India
in order to court death in a manner equally
foolhardy.
Thousands of hard working men are daily
courting death in a much more certain
form, without ever leaving their native vil-
lages or cities. They are the men who neg-
lect their healtn. They are the men who
court death from consumption, or some
other deadly disease due to improper or
insufficient nourishment. The man who
suffers from bilious or nervous disorders,
who has a weak stomach and an impaired
digestion, who has lost the power to eat,
rest or sleep, and who fails to take prompt
steps to remedy these conditions, is court-
ing death in the guise of some fatal malady.
Dr. Pierce s Golden Medical Discovery
cures 08 per cent, of all cases of bronchial,
throat and laryngial affections that lead up
to consumption. It soothes the cough,
facilitates expectoration and restores the
lost appetite. It corrects all disorders of
the digestion, makes the assimilation of
the life-giving elements of- the food perfect.
It invigorates the liver and purifies and en-
riches the blood. It is the great blood-
maker, flesh-builder, nerve tonic and re-
storative. It is the best of all known med-
icines for nervous disorders. Dealers sell
it and have nothing else "just as good "
" I had a had cough and got so low with it that
I could not sit up." writes Mrs. Mittie Gray, of
New London. Union Co., Ark. " Our family
physician told my husband that I had consump-
tion. I had pains through my chest and spit up
blood. I took your ' Golden Medical Discovery '
and it cured me. It saved my life."

WHERE IMPROVEMENTS ARE NEEDED.
AT the meeting of the Board of Supervisors on
Monday Supervisor Holland introduced a
resolution calling the attention of the Mayor
and the members of the board to the numerous de
lays in the completion of the contract for the con
struction of the Hall of Justice building, and also to
the alleged discrimination in street improvements
- been practiced against that portion of ' the
civ lying cast of Kearny street and north of Cali
fornia.
The subject is one which merits all the attention
and consideration it is likely to receive. The delay
in the construction of the Hall of Justice is an old
subject of complaint In fact, it constitutes some
thing like a public scandal, and it is to be regretted
there is no way of indicting some penalty upon the
- who have been and are responsible for it.
That, however, is but a minor factor in the general
problem of providing improvement for that impor
â  ;ection of the city.
There may have been some discrimination in favor
of other districts in making public improvements, but
even if so Supervisor Holland is not justified in at
tributing it to anything else than the apathy of prop
â . ners in the district alleged to have been neg
lected. There is. of course, a continual struggle for
street improvements of all kinds, in which all sections
of the city take part, but as a rule the improvements
are made where the property-owners are most ener
getic in striving for them.
The trouble with the section of the city lying east
of Kearny and north of California is that it is under
going a transformation. It was once the center of
ss activity, and a large proportion of its build
ings were constructed to suit the retail trade and the
cftice work of what San Francisco was twenty years
ago. That sort of patronage has now deserted its old
quarters and sought the newer buildings and better
facilities that exist elsewhere. The old district re
mains to be fitted for the growing wholesale trade,
and when buildings well adapted for that purpose are
[i ted there, and the streets put in good con
dition for the heavy traffic of wholesale business, the
property will yield better profits than ever before.
It is certainly right to expect the city to do what
it can to improve that as well as other sections, but it
right to expect the property-owners to do
something for themselves. That district must be al
ways valuable. The retail trade may shift with the
passing years from one location to another, but the
wholesale trade is fixed to the water front by the
tions of its business. It will of course be
1 benefit to the whole city to have a well built
up and well paved wholesale district, and there will
rdial sympathy with any effort made to accom
plish it.
JUDICIAL CONFIRMATION.
IN The Call of Monday last the view was expressed
that the case of Hunt vs. Ward, decided by the
Supreme Court of this State and much discussed
by the bar as well as by the general public, could
be reconciled with the proposition that the consti
tutional and statutory liability of stockholders for
their proportions of bonds issued by street railway
companies would accrue when the bonds matured
and not when they were issued, and, therefore, that
the outstanding bonds of the Market Street Railway
Company and of the six pre-existing corporations
that formed parts of its consolidation had not been
divested of their essential protection.
In the case of Johnson vs. Bank of Lake, just de
cided by the Supreme Court, this opinion receives
substantial confirmation. That action was brought
to enforce against the defendant, itself a corporation,
its persona! liability as a stockholder in the Lakeport
Agricultural Association for its proportionate share
of a fee earned by an attorney. In supposed cor
respondence with the law declared in Hunt vs. Ward
it was insisted that the personal liability was complete
when the contract for sen-ices was made, and that as
the action was commenced more than three years
after that date the defendant was protected by the
Statute of limitations. The Supreme Court refuses to
recogiii/e this practical annulment of the State con
stitution and of a provision of the Civil Code, and
holds that there was no indebtedness due from the
Lakeport Agricultural Association to its attorney un
til his services had been performed, and that until
then the stockholders were not personally liable and
the statute of limitations had not begun to run in their
favor. The opinion is written by Mr. Justice Hen
shaw and concurred in by Mr. Justice Temple and
also by Mr. Justice McFarland, who wrote the
opinion in Hunt vs. Ward. In the course of a full
l>ut compact discussion of the record this language is
used: "In each case the time of the creation of the
liability is to be determined by the terms and con
ditions of the contract itself." This proposition ap
plies to street railway bonds, when originally issued,
lor by "the terms and conditions of the contract," if
the interest i^ paid, there is no principal indebtedness
due until maturity.
The judgment of the Superior Court, which har-
Toni/cd with the interpretation that has been fre
quently given to Hunt vs. Ward, was reversed. This
ruling of the appellate tribunal is consistent with
other cases referred to by The Call, to the effect that
the liability of a corporation and the personal liabil
ity of its stockholders accrue simultaneously, and is
reassuring to the holders of the street railway bonds
that elicited our comments, who, if they had to de
pend upon the mortgage securities, the value of
which is certainly decreasing, and upon insufficient
jinking funds, would be in a very precarious situa
tion. It does not, alter, however, in a financial sense,
the legal aspects of the bonded indebtedness guaran
teed and created by the Market Street Railway Com
pany.
CUBA LIBRE.
â¢ *" I "HE order issued to \>ur military authorities to
: ! disarm the people of Cuba has produced its
* legitimate effect. The people are hiding their
: arms and keeping their powder dry. We have them
under all the restraint and vexation of military gov
ernment. They have no voice in the simplest police
regulation, none in the imposition of taxes. The
fragments of Poland are not more cowed under the j
German, the Austrian and the Cossack, than Cuba \
under "the Hag of the free."
; We have quoted already from the official declara- ]
tions binding the United States, made in the speeches
of Representatives and Senators in our Congress one
year ago. These declarations cannot be forgotten.
They cannot be concealed. They cannot be de
stroyed.
Senator Morgan, referring on February 23. 1808. to i
a. former resolution of belligerency, said: "At the
1 time of that declaration the proceeding of extermina
tion by military order that had been instituted in the !
island of Cuba by General Weyler was just in process!
of development. Many of us cast anxious eyes to
the future and saw, or thought we saw. the terrible
disaster that was about to fall upon the Cuban people
in virtue of that military order. Rut the method of
! conducting that war by the Spanish Government
never imposed upon us a requirement that we should
declare that a state of war existed in the island of
Cuba, for we could not reckon among the ideas of
civilized and christianized men that extermination I
of women and children was really a state of war. It
was cruel barbarity."
The Senator then described the substitution of
Blanco for Weyler as Captain General, and of auton- i
omy for "unconditional surrender," and said: "What
has caused this result but the arms of Cuba? What
but Gomez and his soldiers 1 Not the United States
Government. It is the arms of Gomez, his strategy.!
his great genius, followed by men who are willing to I
die for liberty, that have produced this offer on the
part of Spain of autonomy, approximating, if it does
not equal, that which the Canadian Government holds
I under the crown of Great Britain. The Cubans alone
have fought and won the revolution. Mr. President,
there has been in the history of nations scarcely any
event which equals that in its importance, and 1 may
say in its grandeur. The Cubans have fought their
revolutionary war and won it. Nothing remains for
them but to hold out until the proud kingdom of
Spain will be obhged to say, 'In place of autonomy
â ye giant you independence. 1 So the Cubans have
not fought in vain. I would much prefer to witness the
rinal triumph of the Cuban arms and the establish
ment of their independence in the .-pirit that ani
mated our fathers than to have the intervention of
the United State* to assist them as we had the in
tervention of France to assist us in getting our in
, dependence." As far back as 1896 the Senate passed
this concurrent resolution: "That a condition of
public wnr exists in Cuba between the Government of
Spain and the Government of Cuba; that the
friendly offices of the United States should be offered
by the President to the Spanish Government for the
recognition of the independence of Cuba." To this
the House added: "Believing that the only perma
nent solution of the contest, equally in the interest of j
Spain, the people of Cuba and other nations, would
be in the establishment of a government by the choice
of the people of Cuba, it i> the sense of Congress
that the Government of the United States should use
j its good offices and friendly influence to that end."
I These expressions of the House and the Senate were
I quoted and approved by Senators Lodge. Teller.
: Morgan. Thurjton, Foraker and others. Concerning
i Cuban independence Thurston said the American
! people ought to be at their altars, praying for it. "si-
Itntly, solemnly, with their consciences before God."
Senator Stewart, declaring that refusing bel
; ligerent rights to Cuba, which would permit her full
acquisition and use of arms, was helping Spain, said:
"Cuban independence is assured as soon as she has
belligerent rights, so that >he can defend them. It is
said Cuba had no ships. If the United States take
its hands off, the world stands ready to sell her
ships. She has the richest island in the world; she
is practically out of debt, and they would be eager to
sell her ships and munitions of war from every part
of the world."
Concerning the arms then in the hands of the
Cubans, Senator Mason submitted documentary
proof that every rifle had cost the Cubans $200. He
drew a picture of a nation compelled by the denial of !
belligerent rights to pay that enormous price for the
guns with which to shoot its way to independence.
All this was a little more than a year ago. Now
we have taken Spain's place in the military govern
ment of that island, and the American people are
asked to send there an army large enough to com- i
pel the Cubans to disarm and surrender the rifles
which cost them $200 apiece.
We desire to say these things now, before Ameri
can volunteers are righting in the fever-smitten
swamps of Cuba to complete the work of subjugation
which Spain abandoned when she offered autonomy.
The imperialist party in this country wants war in
Cuba. It wants the people goaded into armed pro
test. It wants them provoked into "firing on the
flag," in order that it may make a hollow-hearted ap
peal to American patriotism. But over against this
stand the unbroken line of official pledges, legisla
tive and executive, to the independence and self
government of the island. Close behind these events
walks the Genius of History. Her pen recorded the
name of Lafayette in living light. It wrote that of
Washington in letters that will be read until the last
day of time. It put in words that never die Ameri
can sympathy for the Irish rebels of '98, for the pa
triots of Europe who in 1848 broke thrones and
crowns from Skager Rack to the Adriatic, and for the
Suliote. the Sepoy, the Bedouin, when they struck
blindly for freedom. Let freemen who were reared
and nurtured in the principles of the Declaration of
Independence ask what she is writing while our im
perialists are summoning the sons of the people to
die in putting down a "rebellion" in Cuba.
OILING MONOPOLY WHEELS.
THE recent developments of the methods of the
Standard Oil Company before the Industrial
Commission at Washington, D. C, are of pub
lic importance and interest. The testimony of W.
H. Clark, published in The Call of" the 9th inst., is
a striking exhibition of the power and of the facili
ties of that particular monopoly. Its success in
stifling competition and in adapting its product to
the idiosyncrasies of its customers is unexampled and
almost incredible.
This star witness turned out to be a rich lead of
valuable specimens. He furnished several unique il
lustrations of the suppression of competition as a
preliminary to raising the price of oil. In one place
a timorous dealer was driven out of business by a
threat to reduce the price to one cent per gallon, and,
as a natural consequence, ultimately took refuge in
the poorhouse. In another town, where the oil busi
ness was conducted on leased premises, the Standard
Oil Company bought the property, and. seizing a fa
vorable opportunity, carted away the building. In
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JUNE 15, 1899.
1 stiil another small city the monopoly plied its busi
ness under the disguise of the Shomaker Oil Com
i pany, which lasted after all its rivals had been sub
dued.
The company was singularly fertile in its discrimi
nations, adapted to each locality where its exclusive
' right to sell oil was asserted. When the plan was
| safe it boldly furnished oil to different persons in the j
same neighborhood at the most flexible rates. One
j man could purchase at 7 cents per gallon, while his
| next-door neighbor had to pay o'4 cents. Where it
â  was considered inexpedient to parade favoritism to
; friends and hostility toward enemies or neutrals a
uniform price was charged and differential rebates
allowed. In the town of Newark twenty-five differ
ent figures applied to twenty-five classifications of
customers were used.
But tke greatest success of the company was in its
i imitation of the tricks of the prestidigitateurs. One
eccentric character at Newark would buy no oil that
, did not come from Cleveland. The word "Cleve
j land" was painted on a red cask and he was accom
modated. This reminds us of the story of a well
known gentleman in Oregon who received a large
sum for a Durham cow. and. when twitted with the
fact that the cow was of very common blood, inno
cently replied that he had supposed her to be a Dur
ham cow because he had bought her from a man
! named Durham
In many of the Standard Oil stores in Ohio Mr.
I Clark said that the process of supplying different j
qualities nf oil was simplicity itself. Eight gra#es
' were sold out of two tanks, and to change the grade
i* was only necessary tr> turn the faucet in contrary
directions. The miracle of the conjurers who pour
: out wines of different colors and qualities from the
j same bottle was reproduced as one of the features of
! the extensive trade that has made the philanthropy
; of Mr. Rockefeller apparently as inexhaustible as his
; income and his capital.
The narrative of Mr. Clark, which includes various
facetious adulterations of a standard article, strikes
the average Californian as familiar in principle
though new in some of its facts. The railroad dis
criminations, which converted each station into a
custom-house, where duties alternated between rev- j
enue, protective and prohibitory tariffs, have been
understood and appreciated in this State for many
years. The second-hand cars and sleepers that have'
illustrated the economical administration of rail
road business, made possible by the absence or the
; destruction of competition, have induced frequent
contrasts between the West and Hast. The treat
ment of passengers and of freighters has included
every form of rudeness and urbanity, through which
Â the blessings and the curses of a soulless monopoly
could be distributed, without any distinction between
Mount Gerizim and Mount Ebal.
We suspect that there are points of resemblance
between Mr. Rockefeller and Mr. Huntingdon, and
it is deeply to be regretted that their beneficent simi
larity is incomplete.
THE TURKS AND THE CONFERENCE.
SOME signs of trouble, we are told, are visible
before the Peace Conference by reason of the
appearance at The Hague of a noted agitator
seeking to induce the conference to take some ac- j
tion in favor of Armenia against the Sultan. The I
Turkish delegates are reported to have demanded his
expulsion not only from the city hut from the Neth
-1 erlands. under a threat of retiring from the confer
ence if the demand be not granted.
In that way the old Turkish problem arises once
more to confront European diplomacy and disturb
its dreams of peace. From the very day when it was
made known that the conference would .assemble
there came from Southeastern Europe and from Asia
â  Minor the sound of revolutionists, patriots and agi
tators of all kinds making preparations to appear be
fore the conference witli clamors for redress. The j
speaker for Armenia is but the advance guard of a
host of similar agitators who will swarm at The
Hague unless prompt steps are taken to head them
off.
Some time ago there was organized in the Balkan
states a body calling itself the "Central Macedonian
Committee," which started a movement to bring
force to bear upon the conference to induce it to j
recommend a revision of the Berlin treaty and a re
organization of the Balkan states as a preliminary to
the e>tabli<hment of peace. About the same time
Montenegro began to assert claims for a reversion
of Old Servia, and talked of sending delegates to the
conference to demand the annexation of that state ',
before permanent peace should be made. Prompted, j
â  doubtless, by the general movement around them,
the Albanians broke out and for a while threatened
to cause a war without waiting for the conference.
The Central Macedonian Committee is said to
represent the aspiration of the Christians of Mace- J
donia, Thrace and Turkish Roumelia to escape from
Moslem domination by bringing about the annexa
tion of those territories with Bulgaria, thus raising
that state to one of considerable power. The Bul
garians of course would gladly co-operate with them
. in any efforts they should make for accomplishing
' the union. The forces behind the movement are
i therefore sufficiently strong to constitute a serious
menace to the existing situation.
The promoters of alj these movements in the
T!.ilkans niade the mistake of beginning too early, or
at least of announcing their intentions too early. In
formation of what was under way was conveyed to the
Czar, and he promptly took steps to prevent the pur
posed disturbance of his conference. By bringing
pressure to bear he stopped all proceedings so far
as Bulgaria. Macedonia. Montenegro and Albania
are concerned, but it seems he overlooked Armenia,
' and now a cause of disturbance comes from that
quarter.
As this is a world in which it is the unexpected that
happens, it would be quite in accord with the natural
order of things if the proceedings of the Peace Con
ference should produce so much disappointment in
the Balkans as to arouse a spirit of universal revolt
in that part of the world and thus precipitate the long
threatened war that is to involve all Europe. Certain
it i> that with these ominous warnings, sounding a
muffled accompaniment to the peace music at The
Hague, the wary war lords of the nations are not
i likely to agree to disarmament or even to the set
tlement of all international questions by arbitration.
Harry Fisher, a Brooklyn welter-weight, was hit
in the breadbasket Monday night by "Mistah" Joe
Walcott. The press dispatches announce that he
threw up a sponge. It's a wonder he stopped at that.
Professor Hyslop announces his belief that there is
j life after death. To demonstrate his theory he will
j make various tests on departed subjects. The Barnes
, Senatorial boom would about fit his case.
The gas company has again shown its solicitude
for the public. By cheapening the price of the illu
minant it has placed death by carbon monoxide poi
soning within the reach of the poorest.
The conference of charities is seeking a solution of
the tramp problem. Why not try soap factories?
MASCOT OF THE
20TH INFANTRY
ARTHUR GLI'CKMANN, thirteen
years of age. who had the dis
tinction of being the "mascot"
of the Twentieth Infantry, re
turned from Manila on the transport
St. Paul yesterday and is now at the
family residence, 2126 Bush street. He
was wounded in the left knee by an
insurgent bull^j; and confined in the
hospital for seventeen days.
He is a bright, manly little fellow
and tells his experiences with the army
at the front with hearty outbursts of
laughter. His being wounded and the
fact that he was introduced to Ad
miral Dewey are to him as ordinary
occurrences. His story is best told i
his own words:
"I left San Francisco for New York
last September with the First New
York Volunteers. I had a desire to see
the world and thought that was the
beat way to do it. I camp back here on
January 26th last and left on the same
day with the Twentieth Tnfantry on
the transport Scandia. now the War
ren. The hoys immediately made me
their 'mascot.' and treated me splen
didly. My father and mother. did not
know where T was, so when we arrived
at Honolulu I wrote to them saying 1
was going to Manila.
"I saw action at Caliente. Malolos,
Fagbasc River, r a lumplt and nt Appal
lette. which was tlie last engagement
of the Filipino war. It was at Callente
that r was wounded. On March 16 we
were suddenly attacked by the insur
gents and we were told to lay low.
The bullets were whistling around us
pretty thickly and we were given the
order to advance by the right flank. I
suddenly discovered that I had lost
my revolver and remarked to a man
next to me that he would have to give
me his if T was disabled. We came to
a small river and I got on a soldier's
back and got across. While we were
running ft bullet plowed up the ground
a foot away from me. and some one
said it was a pretty close shave. I an
swered "It was all right.' and at that
moment I felt a sharp sensation in my
left leg .iust above the knee, and 1
knew I had beeÂ»i hit. Here Is the
bullet hole in my trousers now. Dr.
McCollum cut out the bullet and a few
days later the chaplain of the Thir
teenth Minnesota presented it to me as
a memento.
"I had anotner narrow escape on March 15th. "WV had crossed the Pasig
River and were ;?oing up trie road when a man was shot right alongside of me.
I went into a house t<> Investigate a little, and when I opened a window
twenty rifles from as many American BOldiers wei leveled at me. They took
me for a Filipino. The major doctor of the regiment ylled out 'Don't shoot;
it's our mascot!'
"I had the pleasure of shaking hands with Admiral Dew ey, and there is
one thing I would like to say; his pictures don't look a bit like him. They
never get his mustache just right. I slept on tin- flagship on thp night of May
Ist. the anniversary of thÂ«- big enga^prr.^nt. you know. The next day Dewty
took me oujt in his launch. I forg n to say that when he first met me he
said, 'Who is this little lad? 1
"They told him I was the mascot of the Twentieth Infantry, and he re
plied: 'I am very glad to meet you.' "
Master Gluckmann is already making plans for the fir-ire and unless pre
vented by his parents will likely carry them out. He saya that he Intends to
work for Douglas Young, the United States agent for the Manila Freedom,
who is now in this city.
The mascot, clad in his rf-gimental p. took his little sister to the Orpheum
last night and attracted a great deal of attention.
A PROTEST IN THE NAME OF TRUTH
Addressed to Governor Roosevelt and all who support the policy of which he
is so distinguished an advocateâ "ln the tropical, withering heat of the Philip
pines, fighting for the glorious flag wilh the .same motive you Grand Army men
fought with in 1864, are your sons find their comrades." Taken from Governor
Roosevelt's speech at General Grant's tomb at Claremont, New York. May 30, 1599.
L'rg*-, if you will, the vowth of this broad land
K< â i.-kleas the ora, familiar way to tread
The blood-stained path of conquest seeking gain.
But pluck no laurels from our patriot dead.
Theirs was no trader's greed; no thirst for rule;
In the fierce heat of fratricidal strife
They faced a foe as valiant as themselves
And gave their own to save a nations life.
For the bright hope of human brotherhood.
Creed â  â ( brave hearts in every clime to-day.
The "ethics of the jungle" substitute
And ape the tiger eager for his prey.
Stil! prate of glory while you kill for gain;
The mighty work our fathers wrought undo;
Incito the strong to trample on the weak;
Oppress the many to enrich the few.
Send forth, like vultures scenting carrion.
Our hosts to slay beneath a tropic sun.
But let them claim no kin with those who fought
To free the slave, to keep our country one.
Los Angeles, Cal.

A PROFESSIONAL LITIGANT.
T. Fox Knocked Out in His Suit
Ag-ainst John Mackay.
The Supreme Court does not take much
stock In the position assumed by Theo
dore Vox against John W. Mackay et al.
In fact, it practically brands him as a
"professional litigant" and upholds the
judgment of the lower court, which was
adverse to the plaintiff. Fox brought this
action as a dissatisfied stockholder and
on behalf of the Consolidated California
& Virginia Mining Company. Mackay and
John P. Jones were the real defendants.
By the bill conspiracy is charged against
Mackay and Flood, the latter now de
ceased, against the company. The fraud
alleged consisted of the purposely care
less milling of ores so as to defraud the
company. In the lower court the findings
of fact were strong against the plaintiff,
and it was upon an assault upon the
findings that the case reached the Su
preme Court. The latter tribunal, In the
decision handed down yesterday, says:
"Defendants also urged "certain 'grounds
is fatal to plaintiff's cause of action. It
was claimed that plaintiff was not a
stockholder upon the books of the mining
corporation at the times these various
frauds were alleged to have been com
mitted. It is said that the record dis
closes plaintiff to be a mere speculator
and professional litigant, making this
class of litigation a specialty, ana that
he purchased these five shares of the
stock of the corporation for the sole pur
pose of giving him a status as a party
plaintiff, and that therefore he has come
into a court of equity with bad motives.
*.â¢;.:* The record largely justifies the
claim of defendants as to the somewhat
equivocal position plaintiff occupies in this
case."
A similar case by Fox against Mackay
and Jones, alleging fraud in the milling
of a certain lot of ores, was similarly dis
posed of. â¢

PRESENTATION CONVENT
GRADUATING EXERCISES
Four Young Ladies
Presented With
Diplomas.
THE thirtieth annual commence
ment exercises of the Sacred
Heart Presentation Convent were
held yesterday afternoon at the
school edifice on Taylor and Ellis
streets.
The hall had been prettily decorated
for the occasion and seated on onf/pide
of the ptagre were the young misses
who had finished their collegiate
course. They were Miss Evelyn Drady,
MiSs Edna Montgomery, Miss Julia
Doody and Miss Josephine Creem, and
formed a pretty picture in their dainty
white costumes. On the other side of
the stage were the scholars of the
lower classes who expect some day to
receive their diplomas.
Very Rev. Vicar General Prender
gast represented the Archbishop and
presented the diplomas and prizes to
the young ladies. He delivered an
address to the graduating class in
which he referred to their having fin
ished their school work, but the more
Important consideration of their life
work was now In order. He cautioned
them to remember the teachings of the
good sisters who had labored tirelessly
in their behalf, and also urged them
to remain true to the principles of
their faith.
The other clergymen present were:
Fathers C. A. Ramm, J. B. Hannigan,
George BurkharHt. S. J.. T. Kirby, P.
G. Butler, S. J. ; M. Commerford and
P. Clancy.
The Presentation Sisters of Berkeley,
Sonoma, Gilroy and the Powell-street
institution were also in attendance.
An interesting feature of the exercises
was the rendition of the operetta
"Genevieve," in which the young
misses acquitted themselves and to
those who instructed them in the va-

HOW THE PALACE
MIXED UP IN TAR
FLAT POLITICS
New Power in the Old
Twenty- Eighth.
MAXWELL'S POLITICAL NIGHT
A CIVIC HERO IN WHITE VEST
AND HIS CIRCUS.
The Results of a Dash for Good Gov-
eminent on the Unterrifled
Democracy Beyant the
Palace.
"When the old Twenty-eighth, awakes to
campaign glory the campaign may be de
clared open. It hag wakened and it's a
three-ring circus. Some time ago the
Buckleyites got a strong club going under
the leadership of Larry Conlon.
A few days ago Gavin licNab derided
that it was time to organize the "decent
element" with which to win the district.
J. D. Maxwell was picked out for chair
man and Michael Brown for secretary.
When Mr. Maxwell was consulted he
would say. "Mr. McNab thinks this," and
"Mr. McNab says that."
Now J. D. Maxwell is a nice and good
man. He is a prominent insurance man,
lives in the Palace Hotel, and is a stran
ger to the turbulence of primary politics
on Tar Flat. After reflecting on the du
ties of citizenship and the political millen
nium which the primary law has ushered
in he agreed that it was nis duty to take
a brave hand in local politics in the inter
est of good government.
He realized that he could be a power
for civic righteousness in the Palace
Hotel where several Democrats reside. He
worked on James V. Coleman and Joe To
bin, and they agreed to also do their
work and hold their places in primary
politics. Thus was the Palace Hotel
dragged Into the arena of primary poli
tics at last. It has hummed with politics
before and politics sizzle there now, but
primaries have been as strange to it as
st'-am beer. It has never thrilled with the
joy of barricaded ballot boxes. It is said
that some of the Democrats of the Palace,
with the grace of the Stratton primary
I,'iw in their hearts, proposed to have one
precinct polling place in the hotel itself as
a striking object lesson on the regenera
tion of municipal politics.
Larry Hoey of the Committee of One
Hundred was brought into the prelimin
aray conferences, as was John Ferris of
the Assessor's office, where Mr. McNab is
credited with Influence. One of the pol
itical powers In the Twenty-eighth is John
F. MeOovern. long secretary of the Com
mittee of Four Hundred and Fifty and
now wharfinger at the Main-street wharf
in his district. He represents a large ele
ment, and in consultation was assured
that only complete harmony was wanted
and that the interests of his faction would
be fully recognized. Notices for the meet-
Ing at Second and Folsom streets were
sent out a w>-ek ago yesterday evening.
Meantime Mr. MeGovern and his friends
decided that it would be just as safe to
have some voting strength on hand, and
workers were sent out to "round up the
gang." The organizers did likewise and
the little hall was packed. Heroes of
many a primary were there in numbers.
Larry Hoey called the meeting to partial
order and J. D. Maxwell was quickly
nominated and elected temporary chair
man per programme. The new political
leader came, out on the little stage in a
white vest and other correct evening at
tire and spoke feelingly of the duties he
and they owed to themselves, to their
city, to the country and to Thomas Jeffer
son.
"Who's this dude that comes down here
wid a white vest to teach us politics?"
growled some of the unterrified.
"I move you. sir. that the chair appoint
a temporary secretary," said John Ferris,
quickly.
"Nof^No!" and "Second the motion!
came the roar.
Now herein appears a trick in primary
rious numbers. The musical selections
on piano, organ, mandolin, guitar and
banjo came in for a large share of ap
preciation, and in fact the whole pro
gramme, which was as follows, was
of a very high order of merit:
Duet "three pianos)
