Chinamen May Not Be Naturalized.
Port Collector Jackson has been notified
of the following ruling of the Treasury j
Department:
"You are advised that it was held In re
Gee Hip <7 Fed. Rep. 274) that a native of j
China of the Mongolian race is not en- !
titled to be admitted to naturalization; j
that a certificate of naturalization issued
to a Chinaman is void on its face and j
that a passport issued by the Department i
of State is not evidence that the person i
to whom it was issued was a citizen of
the I'nited States."

favor of the plaintiff for the sum of $1.

CANNERY TRUST MAY
SOON BE REALIZED
OPTIONS ON TWO-THIRDS OF THE
BIG ESTABLISHMENTS.
All That Remains Is Final Consum
mation of the Deal â Some Big
Houses Have Not En
tered the Combine.
Trust upon trust is the order of the
business world to-day. The newest syn
dicate about to be formed on this coast
embraces the major number of big can
nery establishments in this State.
For the pact few months Chickering.
Thomas & Gregory have been at work
perfecting the outlines of a cannery trust '
and their efforts have so far met with !
success, for they have secured on behalf
of their principals an option on two
thirds of the canneries in this State.
Those establishments which have come
into the combine are: Fontana & Co.'s
San Francisco. Healdshurg and Hanford
cannery: Cutting Packing Company's
three canneries, .situated In this city,
Santa Rosa and Santa Maria; King-
Morse Canning Company's two canneries,
situated in this city and San Leandro;
Sacramento Packing Company's three!
canneries, situated in Sacramento, Yen- |
tura and Visalia: Balfour-Guthrie's two!
canneries, situated in Biggs and in Oak- i
land; Oakland Preserving Company's two
canneries, situated in Oakland and Mil
pitas; San Jose Packing Company's two
cannerieF, situated in San Jose "and in
Marysville; the Carquinez Packing Com
pany' * cannery, situated at Benlcia; A. ;
F. Tenney Company's cannery, situated!
at Fresno; Hunt Bros. Packing Com- j
pony's cannery, situated at Hay wards;
Rose City Canning Company's cannery.
Situated at Santa Rosa; Russian River \
Packing Company's cannery, situated in ;
Healdsburg; Sebastopol Packing Com
pany, situated in Sevastopol; Napa Vai- ;
ley Packing Company, situated in Napa,
and Hood Canning Company, situated in
Emeryville.
There are a number of canneries which
have hold out and have so far refused to
join hands with the combination. Their
output reaches a big portion of the year
ly supply and they will figure as potent
rivals to the combination should they per
sist in their present declination to make
one of it.
The annual output of those canneries
which have joined in a trust is estimated
at about 2.000,000 cases, which amounts i
to over 100,000,000 pounds of fruit. The
capitalization of the trust company Is
fixed at $3,000,000. To those canners who
Join the syndicate a lump sum will be
paid and also a proportionate amount of
the capital stock. The organizers of the
trust disclaim any intention upon the part j
of their principals to raise prices. They i
claim that there will merely be a greater
economy exercised in the operation of
the plants and less expense in the pro
ductiaj of canned goods.
Inventor Edison explains how he

THE SITUATION IN WALL STREET.
The circular of Henry Clews says:
"Some surprise has been caused by shipments
of gold during the week, outside the ordinary
course of exchange. The remittances, aggre
gating $5,500,000, have been chiefly made by one
bank, and that institution is the one which
negotiated the settlement of the Spanish in
demnity. Interest has centered more In the
motive of the transaction than in Its Import
ance, for it is not regarded as foreshadowing
any continuous or Important export of specie
Pi r as being of any great significance to the
Juture of the money market, but as being
merely a transient incident. There are various
obvious ways In which the transaction might
be connected with the Spanish settlement. As
Spain was paid mainly through the transfer
ence of American funds held by European
banks, it is not at all Inconceivable how some
of the great foreign Institutions might need
to replenish their stocks of gold depleted by
the operation, or desire to renew the loans from
American banks which they have been required
to liquidate In connection with the war pay
ment, and the gold exports have probably been
largely attendant on BUCh readjustments.
"The crop outlook has not materially
changed. In some sections the weather has
be^n favorable and in others unfavorable, the
general situation consequently remaining about
as it was two weeks ago. Between the recov
ery In the European crops and the decline In I
ours, we may look for not only a reduction j
in the quantity of our cereal exports, as com- j
pared with the last two years, but also possibly j
a lower range of pr - for food products. The j
surplus of exports over imports may continue i
lnrpe â -â  ugh to prevent any Important exports
r f gold for some time to come, but it is reason
able to expect an ultimate return to a normal
condition of our foreign trade balance.
"The declaration of the usual quarterly divi
dend by the American Bus Company removes
a doubt that has- hung over the market for
quite a long period and played Into the hands j
of the bears, and they have used it from time |
to time in the most skillful way to depress
values. The payment of this dividend has suf
ficed to call attention to the Innumerable num
ber of others which will follow in quick suc
cession between now and the first of July, the j
aggregate amount of which Is estimated for this â 
year at about $150.'W>.000â by far the largest on |
record. A very considerable part of this money
is not unlikely to be reinvested by the recipients
In the very properties that pay them. What
stronger recommendation can be made In be
half of these properties than their dividend- !
I'aylns qualities as demonstrated In this way?" ]

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL I
FORECAST.
The pressure has risen slowly over the coun- i
try west of the Rocky Mountains. There are
r." well-marked disturbances over the western
half of the country. The temperature has risen .
about ten degrees over Utah, Nevada and
Eastern California. In the great valleys of
â¢ "alifornia the temperatures are from eleven
to eighteen degrees above the normal. The
weather is favorable for ripening grain and
fruit.
No rain has fallen on the Pacific Coast south
of Fort c 'anbj

hours, ending midnight June 15:

westerly winds on the coast.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW Y'.RK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. June 14.â professional
boardroom traders, whoseeffort Is to keep in the
current of events for a short turn In prices,
were kept in a very unsettled frame of mind !
to-day by the frequent ebb and flow of prices, I
and they were left In a state of confusion '
at the close under the puzzling conflict of
currents In the market. Some rather large In
terests evidently took their profits on the re
cent rlso yesterday, and having done so, were
read) to avow the fact this morning. This
brought active following from outside sources.
and the commission houses were sellers of the ''
general list. But those who sold stocks freely
in the late flurry yesterday were buyers at
the decline for the purpose of recovering their
Fhort contracts, Â»â < that the belated liquida
tion was pretty well absorbed. In fact, the
covering by the shorts and large buying of some '
of the high-priced specialties carried prices gen
erally alter the opening, above last night's
level. But weak spots began to develop, not
ably in Sugar, Metropolitan and the smelting
stocks, the latter on the prospect of labor
troubles, and a general selling movement set
in. Anaconda, Continental Tobacco, P. C. C.
and St Louis, Baltimore and Ohio. Colorado
and Southern first preferred. International
Paper and the steel stocks, which were advanced I
yesterday, were all special points of weakness
at different times during the day. On the
other hand, the Flower specialties, especially
Brooklyn Transit and Federal Steel, were well '
sustained and showed a disposition to advance. j
Sugar, also after dropping err nearly three I
points, developed an aggressive upward ten
dency and rose an extreme six points. Man
hattan was another center of strength. Thii
deterred the bfars from making new ventures
on the short Â»i ie, although the early advances
wc-re not well maintained. The market was
In fact, a waiting one, in the exact sense of
the word, and fell Into extreme dullness late
in the clay. The quick rally of 44 points in I
Metropolitan Street Railway, after dropping I
84. and Shk in Sugar, lifted the level of prices
from the low plane to which they had been
dragged during the afternoon and made the
close comparatively firm. Only a few stocks
showed net gains, fractional net losses being
the rule. The expected dividend declaration
on Northern Pacific common, which was an
nounced shortly after 2 o'clock, gave that
stock some strength. Otherwise, the grain
carrying roads were. Inclined to heaviness on
talk of harm done by the rains. Denver pre
ferred and the Southwestern generally were
conspicuously weak. The banks were rather
more exacting In granting loans called during
the day. and this led to more attention being
given to the outlook in the money market. The
conditions are much easier In London, where
the effect on the stock market was beneficial
and this lessens the prospect of a further Im
mediate outflow of gold.
There was a reactionary tendency manifest
at some points In the bond market, and the
volume of business done was only moderate*
Total sales, par value, $2,545,000. "I
I Government bonds were unchanged.
The total sales of stocks to-day were 418,860
shares. Including: American Hoop, 3700' Am
erican Steel and Wire, 27,300; American Sugar
Refining. 109.900; American Tobacco, 8800; Atch
:.-..n preferred, 14,200; Brooklyn Transit 69,400-
Burlington. 19.200; Colorado Fuel and Iron !
3100; Continental Tobacco, 9500; Metropolitan I
Street Railway, 14,000; Missouri Pacific 6900-
Federal Steel, 40,900; do preferred, 4100; Man- I
hattan Consolidated. 2300; New York Central !
3200: Xorthern Pacific, 3400; Pennsylvania, MOO; i
People's Gas of Chicago. 9500; Heading first i
preferred. 4200; Rock Island, 7000; St. Paul 03 -
400: Union Pacific. 4800; Western Union 4300." '

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, June 14.â The main feature of
| Wheat trading was the general shifting of
I speculation. to the September option. The open
i Ing was steady, September starting* a shade
I lower at 77%#78%C and selling at once at 77% c
] Cables were firm and higher. , ..Heavy rains !
i were reported in the Black Sea, country, but
it was also stated that, the grain In that section
would not be worth cutting. On this statement
; there was moderate support at the opening, but
soon after the market commenced to decline
on selling by local longs. A decline to H%c
in September ensued, when a limited covering
movement steadied the market for a time, gey.
j tember recovering to 78% C.
' The weather was reported much too wet in
i the spring Wheat country, and this helped to
sustain prices for a time, September sagging
to 76Hc. The ciowe was weak at 7Â«%Â»\

ing of July by a leading commission house was
the feature, canning covering by shorts and
keeping prices slightly above yesterday's dur
ing most of the session. The market weakened
somewhat in the afternoon with Wheat. Sep
| tember closed a shade higher.
Good cash demand and the small country
i movement made a firm market for Oats. Week
! ly crop bulletins generally showed, anxiety on
account of rust and rankness. September closed
Vie higher.-

BOSTON wool MARKET.
BOSTON, June 14.â The American Wool and
Cotton Reporter will say to-morrow: The wool
market has continued strong and active during
the past week, although the volume of busi
ness actually transacted Is slightly less than
that of the previous week. More wool could
have bean sold If holders had been willing to
make concessions, and consumers, who have
done most of the buying during the past week,
have In most Instances been obliged to pay full
prices for the stock which they secured. Con
tinued large sales of territory wool are report
ed, some of which were purchased by dealers.
In the country there Is as much as ever and
prices are advancing all along the lln?. For
eign wools are strong but quiet.
The sales of the week In Boston amounted to
4,475.600 pounds domestic and 504.000 pounds
foreign, making a total of 4,979,600 pounds,
against a total of 1 426,000 pounds for the cor
â¡ding week of last year. Sales since Jan
uary 1, 1899. ; amounted to 111.*>69,800 pounds,
against 52,869,310 pounds last year.

NOTEâ Tn the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
nanrt column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of tlie day. except where there are but
three tirtej. as sometimes occurs. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on tne
United States Coast Burvey charts, except
when a minus sign (- 1 precedes the height
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
pference is the mean nf the lower low T\-;ite. r;i

PROFESSOR FERDINAND STARK DEFENDS HIMSELF
AGAINST A DRESS SUIT AND TWO TAILORS
T->ROFESSOR FERDINAND STARK
has a new dress suit, but STARK
has a new dress suit' but he does
J not like it. The coat does not lit
In the back, he says, and the vest
â hows a strange, and unaccountable
reluctance to meet the trousers half
way. There are also other defects,
all of which the professor pointed out
to Justice of the Peace Barry yester
day afternoon In defense of such of his
acts as have grown out of his dislike
for the suit, and to the confusion of
the tailors who fashioned it. On the
other hand, the tailors express a mod
est degree of pride In the garments
they have made to cover the manly
form of the professor. Incidentally,
they want the professor to take the
suit and .-give them $55, which sum,
they, assert, would be a beggarly but
satisfactory, recompense for their la
bor. At the same time they ask that
the professor be compelled to pay the
additional sum of 12 50 In return for
labor expended In reinforcing the back
breadths of his very-day trousers. To
enforce their demands they found It
necessary to enter suit, and It was this
suit that engaged the attention of
Judge Barry and a number of Inter
ested spectators yesterday afternoon.

& Leger, the complaining tailors, ex
plained to the Judge the details of the
verbal contract whereby Professor

SCHOOL BOARD
IS SCORED BY
DIRECTOR HEAD
Objects to Star Cham
ber Sessions.
The Board of Education at its meeting
laÂ«t night v.iis treoted to an artistic
"rofnÂ«t!ng" at tho bands of Director Head.
i He termed tlio executive sessions of the
hoard "dark lantern sessions" and very
strongly intimated that the sessions were
secret in order to facilitate the transac
tion of "crooked work.'
Practically the entire business of tho
present Board of Education has up to the
present time been transacted behind
closed doors. This has given rise to much
complaint and many whispered accusa
i tions, and it has come to be generally un
derstood that the majority of the mem
-I'Â»t> of the board are either afraid or
ashamed to allow the public to sec them
in tho act of determining what course
shall be taken regarding public business.
T-ast evening, a? soon as the minutes of
1 the previous meeting had been read. Su
, perlntendent Webster asked that the
board resolve Its. -If Into a committee of
i the whole .-uiil n*> into executive ses
sion. This w;;s too much for Director
1 Head, who expressed himself as follows:
"It has become customary for this
board to resolve Itself Into a commit
! tee of the whole for tho purpose of
transacting the business of tho board
! . in secret session. Our business should
be done it; the open- that is. if we
have anything to do of which we are
1 nol ashamed.
"At the last session of this board it
i went into Becret session and consid
ered bids for the painting of certain
BChoolhouses. The result of that dark
1 lantern meeting was to let the con
tract to a bidder who was not the
i lowest bidder. This action was taken
on the bare statement of one of the
i members of the board that the lowest
bidder was irresponsible. I have since
i been informed that the statement was
not founded on fact.
1 'The taxpayers of this city are enti
tled to know how the business of this
department of the city government is
i conducted, and I protest against these
Becret me. -tings."
Director Head said much more to the
same effect, and taken altogether he gave
the members a very uncomfortable five
minutes.
The contract for painting to which he
i referred was let to C. Brooks, although
A. Gottung was the lowest bidder on two
: of the three schoolhouses bid upon.
The motion to go Into executive session
was put and all members except Director
Head voted in th>' affirmative.

School Department were awarded to the
following firms: Bennett Bros., James a.

& Co., Whitaker .^- Ray Company, Cun
ningham. Curtiss <t Welch, Payot, Up

lantic Brush Company, C. F. 'Weber &

I George H. Fuller Desk Company.

recommended that all bids or lumber be
| rejected on th.- ground that all bids were
; too high. Director Head objected to this
: course, and asked that the bidders be
given an opportunity to be heard. On
motion the bids were re-referred to the
committee.
The contract for furnishing ink and pa
: per gave rise to another controversy. Th^
successful bidder was Payot. I'phum &

he declared to be the best and which was
accepted by the committee at tl 2f>. where
as o!h>r bidders quoted price aw low as 90
; cents. Director Kemp proved his asser
tions by the presentation of letters anil
. other documents. Director Gedge, who is
] a member of the supplies committee
sweetly repudiated the Inference that '
there had been any favoritism shown, and
after a long wrangle the. report of the
committee was adopted, Directors Head
and Kemp voting no.

Little Hope for Perry.
Frank Perry, over whose shooting at 107
Guerrero street there remains an en
shrouded mystery, is very low at St.
Luke's Hospital. His condition yesterday
assumed a more Krav> turn, so much so
that the physicians at the hospital have
but slim hopes of paving his life.
The United States a Power for Good.
A distinguished historian writes, while re
ferring to this nation's advent as a colonizing
power, that we represent the "century's polit
ical conscience," and that our influence for
good over European spheres will be Immense.

HE overdue British tramp stearoev Port Albert reached Puget Sound in safety yesterday. She left here
1 for Seattle on June 4 and should have made the run in five days easily. She ran into a nor'wester, how
ever, soon after leaving this port and part of her machinery was disabled. For twenty-four hours she lay
rolling gunwales under in the trough of the sea while repairs were being made, and then when that was ac
comphshpd she had to proceed under a slow bell. The Port Albert will be used as a freight boat in the Seattle-
Honolulu trade.

REUNION OF FORMER
TUOLUMNE RESIDENTS
ASSOCIATION WILL MEET AT
SONORA ON SATURDAY.
Arrangements Perfected for a. Gr?nd
Celebration of the Thirty-First
Anniversary of the Or
sranizÃtion.
j no rommiuce oi arrangements or the
Tuolumne Reunion elation held a
meeting las.t night and perfected the final
plans for its grand ration at Sonora
on Saturday, June 17. The association
has been giving these reunions ever since
1868, when the anniversary, which falls
on the .same date as that of Bunker Hill,
Wfis celebrated in Oakland.
Chairman L. L* Nelson announced that
fifty Tuolumnites ad left this city yes
terday afternoon for Sonora. One hun
dred will leave this morning and over 300
are going to-morrow evening, _ accom
panied by a military band. A committee
will rome down to Stockton to meet them
and on arriving at Sonora the citizens of
Tuolumne will give the visitors a rousing
reception. A chorus of 100 young ladies,
Knyly attired in white dresses, will sing
a welcome song especially composed for
the occasion.
The programme for the day in Sonora
includes a grand barbecue at Marengo
Park. Then? will be literary and musical
exercises, Bides open-air concerts and
dancing day and night. The music will
be furnished by the SoulÃbyvllle and
Sonora bands. a parade composed of the
civic societies, the school children and
members of the association will form at
the old opera hall at 9 o'clock in the
morning and march to the park.
The county Supervisors have contrib
uted a substantial sum to insure the suc
cess of the celebration.
The transportation committee, consist-
Ing or" United States Marshal Shine, L. L.
Nelson and Mrs. C. P. Ford, has made ar
rangements with the railroad for reduced
rates from specified points, the round
trip fare to Honors having been placed
at $7, with return limit until June 30, 1899.
It is calculated that there will be over
2000 visitors to Sonora and it will be a
gala day in the history of the county.

SUPERVISORS MANDAMUSED.
Suit Filed to Compel the Auditing of j
Jurors' Demands.
A petition was filed yesterday by Attor- I
ney Louis P. Bondman for a writ of man- I
date to bo directed against the Board of j
Supervisors, compelling them to audit the j
demands of the various jurors who have j
been sitting- in the criminal departments j
of the Superior Court lor the past several j
months. Under a recent decision of the '
Supreme Court defining the status of civil ]
jurors and holding that they could not j
collect fees for sitting in civil cases the
Supervisors found excuse to refuse to i
audit the demands of the criminal jurors. j
In consequence they organized, employed i
Louis P. Boardman to represent them, j
and suit has now been filed.
Many of the jurors nave been in forced
attendance for many weeks and in con
sequence have been deprived of moneys
they could have made In their vocations. I
Heretofore they have been paid $2 a day, j
and the refusal of the Supervisors to j
allow their demands is working a great }
hardship. Hence the suit will be pushed |
to a final adjudication. j

HON. T. G .PHELPS LAID TO
REST BY SORROWING FRIENDS
ALT, that was mortal of Timothy
Guy Phelps, the distinguished
Californian, whose demise was
hastened by the carelessness .of
bicycle riders, .was borne to the grave
yesterday, surrounded by those who
loved and esteemed him. Ide cere
monies at the home of the deceased
in San Carlos and in the chapel in
peaceful and beautiful Cypress I. awn
Cemetery were of the greatest solem
nity.
The bier was placed in the front room
and was surrounded with a wealth of
blossoms, sent by thoughtful friends.
Dr. Pardee escorted the ( widow to a
seat near the remains and following
them came Mr. and Mrs. J. i->. Schroe
der, Mrs. J. M. Lord, Miss Eugenic
Hawes, Mr. and Mrs. Charles S. Bwa
sev Mr. and Mrs. Edwin McLean,
Mrs. C. E. Dwindle, Mr. and Mrs.
William Chipman, Mr. and Mrs. S. N.
Chipman, Miss Fannie and Miss
Alice Chipman, E. T. McLean, Mr.
and Mrs. Willis Witter and Dr. Wit
ter. â¦
Rev. Dr. Horatio Stebbins conducted
the services. He opened the latter by
feelingly saying: "Friends, neigh
bors and fellow citizens: First of all
Senator Perkins will speak .such words
as seem to him fitting- to the occa
sion, to the hour and to our senti
ments." Senator Perkins, who was a
dear friend of the deceased, then arose
and delivered a eulogy that brought
tears to the eyes of his listeners and
which Impressed every one with the
worth of the late Mr. Phelps. Fol
lowing isa transcript of his, remarks:
"Friend after friend departs,
Who has not â  lost., a -friend?
There Is no union here- of â  hearts
That finds not here an end."
It is as natural to die as It Is to lie born
and â¢to live. Death enters the palace as
well as the cottace. The Rreat ami ] the
learned, the philosopher and the peasant,
the rich and the poor all must obey, its
command. It comes into our social and
business life every day, and .some friend
or acquaintance, is called, to- that .bourne '
whence no traveler- returns. Yet 'we never
become reconciled to Its visit, and we can
not understand why this or that friend
should he taken and we left for a time
to struggle on.
We come to-day to pay the last. sad. trib
ute of respect to the Vnemory of one who
has contributed as much toward develop
ing: the resources and building; up of . our
commonwealth as any other pioneer of- our
State.
Timothy Guy Phelps was born in ; the
State of New York December 24, 1824. . He
received a good common school education,
and when 21 years of age went to New
York to engage In mercantile pursuits, and
Hales.
after .a few years' experience' returned to
Ills rural home to study law. A- few years
later, when the report of gold discovery
in California. â  reached New York, Mr.
Phelps abandoned his law studies and
started for California, arriving in San Fran
cisco December 14, 1849, alter a passage of
162 days from Panama, a length of time
almost equal to the time it would have
required to come by sailing vessel around
Cape Horn. He started immediately for
the mires and engaged in river mining in
Tuolnmne County. Failing, like thousands
of others, to realize his. dream of wealth
from the | new El Dorado, hi?, returned to
San Francisco and embarked in the mercan
tile business, being moderately .successful
until a disastrous fire swept over the city,
taking away the accumulative earnings of.
years of himself and ,â¢ business associates.
Hut with that force and â  energy which were
such strong traits of his character, he did
not become, discouraged,' but applied him
self â¢ with renewed energy to building, up
a new fortune. His public 'political career
began In 1854, when he ran as a candidate
for the Legislature In company with the
late Colonel E. D. Baker, who while a
Senator of I the . United States . from Oregon
cave his life in defense of our country. In
1858 Mr. Phelps was 'nominated and elected
Hales.
to . the . Legislature from Van Francisco and
Ban Mated County on the first Republican
ticket in this State. During the five years
that he was in the Legislature he was al
ways the stanch friend of the people and
of the public welfare. â  He strongly opposed
the bulkhead scheme, a scheme to give pos
session to private speculators of the San
Francisco water front for fifty years and
the right to levy tolls and wharfage upon
the shipping of the port.
In'lSCl Mr. Phelps was a prominent can
didate for Governor, but was defeated be
fore the convention by' Governor {Hartford.
He, however, was immediately nominated
for Congress and elected by a majority of
16.500.' which was the highest encomium to
his popularity- with the people. He at once
took- a prominent part in Congress, and
among other measures he voted,- for the
abolition of slavery In the District of Co
lumbia. He enjoyed in a - marked degree
the friendship and confidence â¢of President
Lincoln, who consulted him in all -matters
relating to the Pacific Coast. : . â -:
I watched last ' evening the setting sun
as It sank away beneath , the horizon. ! The
beautiful glow upon . the. clouds B settled
Into the ' twilight and the gloaming of the
evening into the somber hue of nieht. It
seemed as if . the sable rail would always
Hales.
envelnp the enrth. But behold the cflange
tiiis bright m iraing, when the dawn pro
claimed the coming of the day and the
mountain peaks and the spires were bathed
in the life-Elvlngr and Inspiring rays of
the king of day. So with usâ so it is with
our dear friend whose bier we to-day sur
round. He will surely live aeain.
The Masonic choir, composed of

"Still, Still With Thee." At the con
clusion of the singing Dr. Stebbins led
in prayer. The aped and eloquent di
vine touched on the present and here
after, sympathizing with the family of
the deceased and paying his tribute in
words of beauty and significance.
After the singing of "Rest in Peace"

hoarse with bared heads were the fol
